B009POCFTG|5.0|Few films contain such a palpable mix of dark comedy and brutality as William Friedkin's Killer Joe. The film reunites Friedkin with writer Tracy Letts, who last worked together on 2006's Bug. That film was a memorable little oddity that polarized audiences. If you have the stomach for it, Killer Joe is the superior film. For Friedkin, whose career was built on successes he had decades ago, it's a roaring comeback. For the star Matthew McConaughey, it's a career renaissance. In the last year, after serving time as the go-to guy for romantic comedies, McConaughey has begun taking on more daring roles and this is his most daring yet. It's also his best performance.<br /><br />Most of Killer Joe takes place in a trailer park somewhere in Texas, home of the Smiths, a family of rednecks for whom the word `philistine' was almost certainly invented. Chris Smith (Emile Hirsch) is a drug dealer whose mother has stolen his stash and left him desperately in debt to some guys who will kill him unless he comes up with $6000. Learning of his mother's $50,000 life insurance policy for which his sister Dottie (Juno Temple) is the beneficiary, Chris approaches his father Ansel (Thomas Haden Church) about the prospect of having his mother killed. Not only would this solve Chris' problem, but it could lead to a big payday for Dottie, Ansel and his current wife Sharla (Gina Gershon). The person to do it is Joe Cooper (McConaughey), a Dallas detective who moonlights as a hitman. After they meet with Joe, he demands $25,000 in advance that Chris and Ansel are unable to provide. But Joe is willing to negotiate and will accept a retainer; Dottie.<br /><br />There's no telling who is working against each other in the Smiths family. Although they lack any semblance of sagacity, their ignorance spawns both humor and horror. There's a sardonic glee in the way their story unfolds; we never know the length of stupidity they're capable of. The only redeeming character is Dottie, who seems mentally slower than her family (quite a feat), yet innocently more aware.<br /><br />Letts' script is an adaption of his own play yet, unlike many stage adaptations, there's never a sense that this was made for the stage. One reason is Friedkin's fearless direction and the other is Caleb Deschanel's unflinching cinematography. Killer Joe was initially rated NC-17 (eventually surrendered) and it's hard to recall a film that so deserves it. This is a violent, bloody, misogynistic, and often sadistic film that basks in its lurid tendency to push the envelope. However, there's much more to Killer Joe than mere shock value. It's self-aware and crackles with great dialogue and memorable performances from everyone involved. It jumps right into the plot but slow burns with quiet intensity before erupting into total chaos, fueled mostly by McConaughey's menacing and frightening performance.<br /><br />Before becoming a romantic-comedy heartthrob McConaughey appeared in Texas Chainsaw Massacre: The Next Generation and for all that film's shortcomings, he did an exceptional job playing crazy. Even with that performance on his resum√©, who could've foretold McConaughey could deliver such a memorable performance that allows him to exploit his natural charisma and charm to play completely against type and make Joe Cooper's ferocity and stark brutality all the more shocking? The performance is so unconventional and horrifying that it won't lead him to an Oscar, but few will argue what an Oscar-caliber performance this is. The other stand-out is Church, playing a character so stupid it takes the right kind of actor to keep it from bubbling over the top. Church's comically silly facial hair and his gift for deadpan make Ansel the comic relief of the film, but he keeps the character from seeming contrived or false within the film's context. He's a gifted actor who never fails to please and surprise.<br /><br />The conclusion of Killer Joe features a long scene involving McConaughey, Church, Gershon, and a chicken leg that stands out as one of the most nail-bitingly intense scenes of any film this year. It's hard to anticipate a scene more startling or suspenseful. Here McConaughey reaches deep into his acting range to show the depths of Killer Joe's (both the movie and character) depravity; this is the moment where he totally sheds the rom-com image. If the Academy were more willing to award edgy, brave performances, this moment would almost certainly win him an Oscar. It's a scene that bristles with energy and no matter how chaotic and shocking it becomes, you can't look away.<br /><br />Killer Joe is a film that gets in your head and stays there. It's a shocking and powerful film that makes a devastating impact. It's certainly entertaining, but make no mistake, it's not for the faint of heart and will disgust and revolt many an audience member. It's one of the most disturbing films of 2012. It's also one of the best.<br /><br />GRADE: A-

I didn't know anyone else who'd seen it.  I thought my sister and I had dreamed it up and it didn't actually exist.  Then we saw it at my cousin's house.  It existed.<br /><br />I absolutely, unequivocally love this movie.  I'm glad that it's found a cult audience despite bombing in theaters.  And yes, it has stood the test of time.  I recently showed it to my niece and nephew and they enjoyed it.  My niece, who is almost five, rarely sits still for an entire episode of Spongebob Squarepants, which is only twenty-five minutes long and her favorite television show.  Hocus Pocus held her spellbound for an hour and a half.  (Okay, her attention did waver a little until the witches showed up.)  After she made sure to tell me how much she liked it.<br /><br />Hocus Pocus isn't your standard Disney film.  For one thing, most Disney films don't make multiple jokes about a teenage boy's virginity.  (Parents, don't worry.  They went over my head and my sister's when we were kids, and recently they went over my niece's head.)  There's even a scene, watching as an adult, that I realized was a G-rated version of masturbation.  And the Sanderson sisters are much scarier than most Disney villains.  Hocus Pocus opens with the death of a young child and another tortured before being cursed.  But that's part of what makes Hocus Pocus so much fun.  It trusts it's audience.  It's more funny than scary, but there's a real sense of darkness that sells the scary parts.<br /><br />Plus, the cast is fantastic. B00006ADFL|5.0|HEAVENLY CREATURES is a true story about two teenage school girls who become friends in early 1950s New Zealand. Now, doesnt that sound sweet. Well its not! Its actually the exact opposite of sweet. It was ...what is the word I am looking for...oh yes, homicidal. Pauline Parker and Juliet Hulme called each other Heavenly Creatures. They shunned the outside world and created a complex and elaborate fantasy world that they shared. Pauline, (Melanie Lynskey), who narrates the film, comes from a lower class family. She feels alienated from both her family and her schoolmates and walks around in a sullen daze until the day she meets Juliet, (Kate Winslet), an upper class girl who outwardly is beautiful and self assured but inwardly alone and ignored by her busy upperly mobile parents. The two compliment each other. They both feel superior to the lives they pretend to lead in humdrum Christchurch, N.Z.. They share a passion for Mario Lanza and the stars of Hollywood. Jackson does a superb job in inviting the audience into the fantasy world of these young girls and the cinematography depicting this world helps us to understand how seductive and enticing this form of escapism truly was to these two. The performances by both Lynskey and Winslet are top notch and along with the great direction by Jackson and creative storyline makes this film a haunting but unique view into the lives of two young, intelligent but insane teenage girls.

B008YZXYY2|3.0|As I have found to be the case with almost every double album I have purchased in recent memory, the second release is always widely regarded as the better half. Why this is, I'm not sure. Perhaps artists save their juciest material for the finale, or maybe there is just something more exciting about hearing the conclusion than the introduction. Whatever the reason may be, Stone Sour's House of Gold & Bones Pt. 1 is no exception. Frontman Corey Taylor boldly predicted last January that this two part concept album could only be described as \\"Pink Floyd's [[ASIN:B000006TRV The Wall]] meets Alice in Chains' [[ASIN:B0000028M7 Dirt]] on steroids.\\" If only it were so.<br /><br />There are only two similarities between House of Gold & Bones Pt. 1 and The Wall: First, House of Gold & Bones Pt. 1 contains a two-part song (could there be a third part on disc 2?)titled \\"The Travelers\\"; second, there will ultimately be a 2nd disc in House of Gold & Bones Part 2. Thematically, the two \\"epics\\" are vastly different. Where The Wall explores a myriad of issues relating to its central character, House of Gold & Bones Pt. 1 acts only as a slightly more focused effort than Stone Sour's previous albums, all of which, in some form or another, speak to the same topics as this album (think: anger, burnout, love, getting-a-grasp-on-one's-own-emotions, etc.). As for resembling Dirt, I am not buying into that at all. As one of my all time favorite albums, I can state with confidence that any similarity between Dirt and House of Gold and Bones Pt. 1 ends at Corey Taylor's comparison. Stone Sour's latest effort does not even begin to capture the frustration, pain, and emotion that Dirt so successfully encapsulated in 1992.<br /><br />Lofty expectations aside (and to be quite honest, I did not put HoG&B Pt. 1 into my stereo expecting to be blown away), HoG&B Pt. 1 still disappoints. If you loved Stone Sour before this release, you will likely still love them after hearing it; if you hated Stone Sour before, you will still hate them now. There are no major surprises on HoG&B Pt. 1. We get a couple heavy rockers in \\"Gone Sovereign\\" and \\"RU486,\\" a natural single and incredibly catchy track in \\"Absolute Zero,\\" a couple mellow acoustic tracks in \\"The Travellers Pt. 1\\" and \\"Taciturn,\\" and a handful of other tracks chalk-full of the type of material fans have come to expect from Stone Sour. There certainly is nothing ground-breaking here.<br /><br />Interestingly, Stone Sour does utilize some strings (\\"Tired\\") on their latest release. Perhaps the track that comes closest to living up to Taylor's prediction of wonder and awe, \\"Tired\\" is immediately distinguishable from its contemporaries by its slow but powerful buildup as well as the inclusion of said strings in the background. Also returning with a purpose are the piano and keyboards (\\"RU486\\"; \\"Taciturn\\"; \\"Influence of a Drowsy God\\"; \\"The Travellers Pt. 2\\") seen sparingly on their previous two releases, [[ASIN:B000G8NXBY Come Whatever May]] and [[ASIN:B00415R59O Audio Secrecy (Special Edition)]], keeping things just fresh enough to entice the listener. Fortunately, there also is not a huge dropoff in bass quality. Although I am not sure if former bassist Shawn Economaki left the band prior to or after the material for this album was written, Rachel Bolan (of Skid Row fame) fills his boots as the resident studio artist on HoG&B Pt. 1 quite well. Pleasantly, and as I have come to expect from Stone Sour, the production on this album is also top-notch.<br /><br />In the end, Taylor's lofty expectations for this double release are just that- lofty expectations. While there is certainly much to enjoy on House of Gold & Bones Pt. 1, specifically the ultra-catchy single, \\"Absolute Zero,\\" its successor, \\"A Rumor of Skin,\\" and the musically formidable middle tracks \\"Tired\\" and \\"RU486,\\" there is also nothing ground-breaking, nor is there anything unexpected. Stone Sour follows a winning formula that we have all heard on previous releases. Bad idea? Not at all. Exciting? Not really. Will it win over any new fans? Unlikely. Because Stone Sour sticks to its guns on this disc, a handful of tracks end up being real sleepers (\\"My Name is Allen\\" is the worst Stone Sour track to date). The bottom line is this: if you are already a fan of Stone Sour, then you know what to expect out of House of Gold & Bones Pt. 1. Personally, I was expecting something edgier than what I got, but I am still enjoying this latest Stone Sour release. I hold out hope that Part 2 will fill my appetite, but Part 1 certainly left something to be desired.evious releases. Bad idea? Not at all. Exciting? Not really. Will it win over any new fans? Unlikely. Because Stone Sour sticks to its guns on this disc, a handful of tracks end up being real sleepers (\\"My Name is Allen\\" is the worst Stone Sour track to date). The bottom line is this: if you are already a fan of Stone Sour, then you know what to expect out of House of Gold & Bones Pt. 1. Personally, I was expecting something edgier than what I got, but I am still enjoying this latest Stone Sour release. I hold out hope that Part 2 will fill my appetite, but Part 1 certainly left something to be desired.

B000ELJBAA|4.0|During an interview for VH1's \\"Legends\\" program, when asked about Pink Floyd's blockbuster \\"The Wall,\\" David Gilmour said \\"It slightly lacks soul for me.\\"<br />And indeed Gilmour had been at odds with founding bassist and then-lead songwriter Roger Waters over the direction of the band's music, live performances, album cover art, temperature of the frozen yogurt machine in Eel Pie Studios, and just about anything else band members can dispute. The Floyd output under Waters' authority had been increasingly dark, but a solo album, Gilmour's second, allowed the heralded guitarist to breathe a bit easier.<br />\\"About Face\\" is certainly more musical than Floyd's most recent album, \\"The Final Cut\\" - but, despite being a brilliant album, even the sound of the frozen yogurt machine shorting out had been more musical than that record. \\"About Face\\" finds Gilmour loosening up, and crafting some impressive pieces, free from Roger Waters' glare. Though the album is definitely dated in places, notably the driving, technologically heavy opener 'Until We Sleep,' \\"About Face\\" finds some of the \\"soul\\" that Floyd's recent work had been lacking, notably in the invigorating, horn-boasting 'Blue Light,' the distinctly 1984 pop-friendly 'Love On the Air,' the gorgeous 'Near the End,' the very un-Floydian 'Cruise,' and the ferocious 'All Lovers Are Deranged.' The ballad 'Out of the Blue' and the commentary on the death of John Lennon, 'Murder,' foreshadow some of the sounds and themes the reunited Pink Floyd would later explore under Gilmour's watch.<br />Released just a month apart from Roger Waters' solo effort \\"The Pros & Cons of Hitchhiking,\\" David Gilmour had obviously fared better than his erstwhile bandmate at branching out creatively. \\"About Face\\" would be the guitarist's final solo album until the hit \\"On an Island\\" in 2006, but it stood up well during those twenty-plus years, a solid album that reflects a musician's creative desires, personally and professionally.

Is the party over for 007 and his backdated brand of boyish bravura?<br /><br />This question is on Sam Mendes' mind too. And 007‚Ä≤s, and M's. This time it isn't just James Bond who is the dinosaur. It's MI6 itself.<br /><br />Previous Bond franchise reboots have been about modernising a diehard chauvinist throwback. That this was quixotic and self-defeating was demonstrated by the effete outputs: Goldeneye. Die Another Day.<br /><br />Sam Mendes addresses that head-on: Daniel Craig is scarred; haggard; careworn. Old. He looks like he's had enough, even before being snipered off the roof of a goods wagon during a punch-up over a high Turkish gorge in the opening scene.<br /><br />To underline the air of finality, as Bond's body hits the water and floats into a truly old school animated opening sequence, a truly old school Bond theme strikes up, and in the fashion of a truly old school Bond chanteuse, Adele sings, to almost exactly the tune of Diamonds are Forever \\"This is the end\\". Good gracious: they're not declaring the innings closed, are they?<br /><br />It's a question that hangs over the two and a half hours of Skyfall.<br /><br />MI6 has carelessly let its roster of embedded under-cover agents fall into terrorist hands, and now they're being systematically whacked in full view of the world wide web. In trying to retrieve the list 007 has been shot, seemingly dead: collateral damage of a risky shot by his own colleague, Eve (Naomie Harris). A risky shot taken at the direct order of M herself, in knowledge of Bond's jeopardy, we fear to protect herself as much as her department.<br /><br />And now the net is drawing in on M herself. Gareth Mallory (Ralph Fiennes), a career bureaucrat, is put in charge of the department. He wants M out in a month. All done the proper way, by dignified retirement: a very British coup.<br /><br />In her eyes she knows her days are numbered, but M is having none of it, not while there's still work to do. Dumbledore repairs to her study where her favourite boy wizard - the boy who lived - awaits. (Forgive me confusing my movie idioms: as the film progresses the similarities become ever more striking)<br /><br />Time to get back to what we do best, James. Old school style. And always, confronting our right to be here: are we still relevant? Are we still understood? In the middle of this social revolution are we still fit for purpose? If we're to best make sure we're not redundant, how should we respond?<br /><br />M's, and Mendes' answer: go back to basics; concentrate on what we know best. Old school.<br /><br />Bond sits in contemplation at the national gallery in front of Turner's painting of the HMS Temeraire, a willowy veteran of the Battle of Trafalgar, being towed by a steampunk tug to be scuttled. He is interrupted by a precocious teenager (Ben Whishaw), who turns out to be the new Q. But this time gimmicks are muted: a palm-print personalised Walther PPK and a miniature radio tracker. Old school. Bond reverts to his old DB5. He's tempted to use the ejector seat on M when she's haranguing him: Old school.<br /><br />Much has been made of Javier Bardem's villain, but this film isn't about the villain. Raoul Silva is no Anton Chigurh; Bardem plays him almost as comic relief. His villainy is barely needed: Skyfall deals in a far more claustrophobic perfidy than him.<br /><br />Things proceed with a sense of scale and grandeur that James Bond has never in my experience attained, for two and a half hours. It feels like there's more at stake than is usually the case; it feels here that, however it ends, the Bond universe will have irreparably changed. And so it turns out. The ending is moving, profound and ingeniously it leaves the franchise ready to embark upon a next decade of adventures, with all the pieces in place.<br /><br />Old school.<br /><br />Olly Buxton, profound and ingeniously it leaves the franchise ready to embark upon a next decade of adventures, with all the pieces in place.<br /><br />Old school.<br /><br />Olly Buxton

0525953728|3.0|\\"No Easy Day\\" has a lot to recommend it - it's a lean, spare book, free from hyperbole and narrowly focused on the life of Navy SEALS.  And one cannot quibble with the choice of subject matter, as the raid on bin Laden's Pakistani compound is the real-life stuff of Hollywood blockbusters.<br /><br />So why the mediocre review?<br /><br />First, because the book is actually too narrow a tale for the full story of the bin Laden mission.  As the authors admit, they really only came in for the tail end of the mission - they pulled the trigger.  This observation does not belittle their achievement in any way, but it recognizes that the soldiers were not involved in the herculean task of discovering where bin Laden was.  \\"Mark Owen,\\" the psuedonym of our hero, meets one of the intelligence officers who actually tracked bin Laden down and we get to see her reaction to the successful raid (one of the more poignant and effective scenes in the book) - something tells me that there is a magnificent epic to be told about the tracking - and the killing - of bin Laden.  Here, we really only get the closing act.<br /><br />Second, while the book offers a lot of details about life as a member of the special forces, it doesn't tell a great story about that life.  Owen says that the life puts a strain on families, but offers only a couple of brief anecdotes to back it up (although the image of a SEAL coming home from a mission, only to be told to change the baby's diaper, was pretty darn funny).  Owen describes training and says it's hard, but \\"No Easy Day\\" is hardly a riveting examination of the training regimen.  It would be like a marathoner saying, \\"I trained for a marathon, I ran it, I'm glad I did, but boy was I tired.\\"  We get a few minor stories about the guys playing practical jokes on each other and a few battle scenes, but I was left wanting more.<br /><br />Full disclosure - I picked up \\"No Easy Day\\" after reading M.M. Kaye's magnificent opus, \\"The Far Pavillions.\\"  Thathuge book, one of the best stories written in the last 30 years, offers more enjoyable details in ten pages than \\"No Easy Day\\" contains in its entirety.  So perhaps I'm being unfair.  However, I wanted to adore \\"No Easy Day,\\" and I cannot say that I do.<br /><br />This should be a story that you want to re-read over and over, and all I can say is that once is enough.
0679731725|3.0|While reading this book I found myself wondering how and why it had been awarded a major literary prize.<br />--The narrator is so dehumanized and oblivious in his responses to virtually everything that it's difficult to empathize with him.  The scenes with the narrator's father and the dysfunctional relationship between the two men give some idea of why the narrator is so stunted emotionally, but that doesn't really help the novel.<br />--The main female character behaves in such a childish and petty way when thwarted that it's difficult to empathize with her.<br />--The endless musings about importance and dignity are reminiscent of the endless musings about the nature of quality in Zen and the Art of Motorcycle Maintenance.  Fascinating to college freshmen after a couple of doobies, but pretty thin support for a novel.<br />--And then there are the supporting cast: the dotty germanophiles, the ignorant villagers, the youthful liberal, the crass American.  Clich&eacute;s all.<br /><br />Halfway through I realized that this book is in some respects similar to Animal Farm.  The characters are unrealistic cardboard cutouts but the novel is more palatable if you regard it as a political parable.  Orwell did it better, and with more originality, but Ishiguro has come up with a different approach, for which he should be given credit.  (On the human and interpersonal level, a film like The Hireling, for example, did a far better job of dealing with the angst and inequity of the class system.)<br /><br />In spite of Ishiguro's somewhat creative approach, I can only go as high as three stars because when the characters are human there has to be something to make us care about them.  It's very hard to get wrapped up with a cast that is so mentally and emotionally limited and deliberately obtuse.  I'd give it two stars, but Ishiguro gets credit for designing a functioning robot and maintaining the same tone and ambience from start to finish.  This literary world is internally consistent--but it's terribly limited and limiting.istent--but it's terribly limited and limiting.

1400067820|5.0|Continuing (and building on) the themes from [[ASIN:1400067936 Fooled by Randomness: The Hidden Role of Chance in Life and in the Markets]] and [[ASIN:081297381X The Black Swan: Second Edition: The Impact of the Highly Improbable: With a new section: \\"On Robustness and Fragility\\"]], Taleb provides a very thought-provoking (and unconventional) look at how systems should be gauged in terms of how they thrive in shocks as a true measure of strength as opposed to traditional risk/volatility metrics.  In a way, the main theme seems to be traditional approaches deny the natural antifragility systems have and we shouldn't manage risk. It is counter-productive.<br /><br />Such a statement, obviously is going to provoke the ire of legions of risk managers and they may have a point. But using a wide variety of arguments leveraging the concept of stressors, domain dependence, causal opacity, hedonic adaptation, Taleb laments the \\"touristification\\" of systems and the Procrustean nature of how we tend to look at systems.<br /><br />Mixing philosophy, mythology, (highly) disrespectful humor, Taleb provides a very intriguing and convincing way to look at systems, particularly how we quantify risk.  He is at his best when he defends the notion of entrepreneurship where he is able to clearly articulate the impact of serendipitous tinkering as opposed to formal education - and this celebration of entrepreneurship takes over a good portion of his arguments.  The discussions on stochastic resonance - why adding randomness can be good - and how the artificial suppression of volatility is bad is very informative. Perhaps the best part of the book is the lucid discussion of optionality - and how one could use options and optionality as the foundation of measuring \\"fragility\\".  that discussion, alone, is worth investing in this book.<br /><br />The verbose narratives, crude sense of humor, frequent jumping-around-topics, and a general lack in editing are all hallmarks of Taleb - this bookis no exception - but that makes the reader pay extra attention to what is being said.  With 500 pages, this is no easy read.  But Taleb does an excellent job of separating out the more technical portions in a way that doesn't impact the flow of his arguments. The long citation list and Taleb's own technical papers based on the themes in this book is likely to keep a curious reader busy for a year or two.<br /><br />This is a book I will re-read every few months or so. Two key concepts - optionality and the general discussion on artificial management of volatility suppressing natural anti-fragility - have made this book a true 'enlightener' for me. Absolute must-read.

0375507256|5.0|Ten minutes after finishing this book I found myself lonely for it, desperate to wear in the binding from chapter one and begin anew.  If it were possible to un-know the contents of \\"Cloud Atlas\\" and discover them again, I would embark on this journey every year for the rest of my life...the powerful images still flit through my daydreams and nightdreams...an aged composer...a stopover in the Chatham Islands...a dystopic ultramodern Korea...a publisher, locked away...<br /><br />This book will enchant the deepest parts of your curious, questioning mind, and if you haven't got one of those, this book will give it to you.  I am not the most well-read bon. lass `mongst the bookish Warrior Womyn class of the Pacific Northwest, but I punch in more than a few nights a week at our City of Books and somehow always make time to hole up and retreat to my page worlds--thus with this trifling credibility I entreat you to read this book; it is probably the greatest work of our century.<br /><br />\\"Cloud Atlas\\" is a genre-bending voyage through times, spaces, cultures, races, political systems, social orders, religious assumptions, genders, and so, so many more of the hinges that humanize us.  The author has described his championing work as a \\"sextet for overlapping soloists\\", in which each solo is interrupted by its successor and in the second, each interruption is continued in order.  The byzantine structure of the book is woven by six overlapping stories, each revealed by not only different characters but also vastly dissimilar flairs of script.  The author graces us with stylistic fluctuations of historical, science, neo-noir, dystopia, farce, and comic fiction, and each takes their shape via diaries, letters, mysteries, interviews, and a tale told around a fire.<br /><br />The dense book is daunting at first, but when you consider that it covers kindness, cruelty, civilization, greed, vengeance, art, history, faith, tradition, love, family, religion, barbarism, enslavement, release, the corrupting and dehumanizing nature of power, the universal cultural story of societies rising, falling, and building again, all told with a stimulating and challenging narrative, it is amazing that David Mitchell could manage to fit a lifetime's (or two or ten's) worth of philosophizing into a mere five hundred pages.<br /><br />Somehow, without my noticing, the book managed to fit ALL of this discourse into an entertaining read that detonated my cognizance towards the minutest of intricacies, but closed on a simple, beautiful theme.<br /><br />The last lines will leave you sated and bursting with a love of man.<br /><br />Surprisingly, the book elicited within me deep stirrings on God and What Is Godly.  The multiple religious constructs presented in the book had me questioning my own lack of questioning in hateful ways, only to feel, at the next chapter, a deep yearning for the conviction expressed by the characters therein and a jealousy of their certainty--which I found amusing given that my region has the lowest rate of church attendance in the United States and consistently reports the highest percentage of atheism.<br /><br />Throughout the book you may notice a handful of blatant references to other films, books, stories, fables, musical passages.  Why would the author appropriate, plainly and in even the least-cultured reader's sight, the characters, story arches, and concepts of 1984, Blade Runner, Nurse Ratched, Soylent Green and other classics?  These references are rife with meaning and are not to be casually overlooked.<br /><br />In addition to confronting tough and unfamiliar language, I had to concentrate very hard just to glean every bit of meaning from the lush passages.  Many key thematic tirades were loaded with double and even triple meanings, and I found myself pawing back and back again just to be sure I had them all.<br /><br />I agree with other reviewers that it was hard to get into Chapter One at first, in fact, all of the chapters were a little bit hard to \\"settle into\\", and they took quite a bit of \\"legwork\\" and guessing from the reader in terms of determining time, place, new characters, etc.  I didn't care for the middle story (the central anchor) much...call me lazy but I don't like invented slang that takes hours to dissect; it feels gimmicky, and his payoff wasn't worth the reward to me as a reader.  The work was well worth it though, and by the time I was 25% through the book I knew I wouldn't be able to eat, sleep, or do ANYTHING until I had finished the entire thing.  Work through the research and enjoy this part of the process...your patience will be greatly rewarded.<br /><br />I predict \\"Cloud Atlas\\" will go down in publishing history as the rumbling cult favorite that spawned the visionary artists, thinkers, and designers of the future.  This book is required reading for anyone who aspires to pass through the ignorance/innocence of childhood and into the realm of adulthood.<br /><br />Take the time to dissect this beautiful gift of a novel and be forever enlightened as to man's capacity for love, hate, good, evil, and maybe, just maybe, deliverance.hapters were a little bit hard to \\"settle into\\", and they took quite a bit of \\"legwork\\" and guessing from the reader in terms of determining time, place, new characters, etc.  I didn't care for the middle story (the central anchor) much...call me lazy but I don't like invented slang that takes hours to dissect; it feels gimmicky, and his payoff wasn't worth the reward to me as a reader. I really do.<br /><br />The record opens with the lyrics: \\"Teenage angst has paid off well / Now I'm bored and old\\" and they set the tone for the entire thing. Kurt's vocal performance is brutally despairing and you can really hear the pain and emotion in his voice. Keep in mind that I am an avid music listener who's heard countless records from every era and nearly every genre. More importantly, \\"Princess\\" was a book with a lot of exposition which can work well enough in a book but is undesirable for a film. Films need a faster pace than a book.<br /><br />2. The Therns. The Therns are certainly much more powerful in the film as compared to the book(s). On the plus side they do provide a convenient explanation for how Carter gets to Mars. This was badly needed as Burroughs was very vague about this. On the debit side they are too powerful and make Carter too much of the pawn and not enough of the all conquering hero that Burroughs created.<br /><br />3. Dejah Thoris. Lynn Collins *is* Dejah Thoris, exactly as a dozen paperback cover artists have depicted her over the decades. Perfect casting. Her character has been updated in two ways to bring her more in line with 21st century sensibilities. First, she is as much a swordsman as Carter whereas in the books her weapon of choice was a dagger used to defend her honor against various miscreants. Second, she is depicted as a scientist here, a role her son played in the novels. Both changes were quite acceptable.<br /><br />4. Jumping ability. Hugely exaggerated over what Burroughs established in the novels. The whole Thark/Tars Tarkas/Sola/Woola plot points survived.<br /><br />The \\"sword and planet\\" genre has been out of fashion for a long time now. It has always been something of a male juvenile genre which one had to be exposed to before age 15 to appreciate. The audience for this film probably died out long before the technology was available to bring to the screen. Those of us who survived to see it can be grateful that Disney brought it to the screen and it is to be regretted that they were not financially rewarded for their fine efforts. Highly recommended.. 0156027321|1.0|This book proposes we choose between a good story and `dry yeastless factuality', the question then arises if the opposite of this would be Moist Yeasty Fallaciousness? And why exactly that would be the better of the two?<br /><br />Let me start by admitting that I loathe Martel's positivity, it comes across as a lithium induced bounciness that only just hides a humidly desperate yearning for significance. And oddly enough, if one takes the foreword seriously that is exactly what it is. There is much I detest about this book, but to be brief I'll just say this:<br /><br />Stories with twist ending are almost always devilishly tricky things to get right, more often than not the clever twist negates what has gone before. The Life of Pi sadly doesn't avoid this pitfall or seemingly even attempts to do so. Martel marches his story right into his own trap.  The deep message of his book is that we can decide what to believe and thus create our own reality. Fair enough, if one can stay five years old. Most of us grow up however and realise that the world is much richer in complexity and indifference than we ever expected. This makes Martel's idea a laughably silly and one dimensional proposition. One merely has to walk out of the upstairs window into a self created reality where gravity is on holiday to see how that little delusion pans out. Reality -like gravity- works whether we acknowledge it or not. The indifference of reality is as vast as that of heaven.<br /><br />In this stumbling twist Martel negates his early premise that this story will make us believe in god. Since he merely presents us not with god as he appears in religious texts, eg. As a the omnipotent creator of the universe who is REAL. But rather as a reduced version of god who is likely not real, but such a nice idea that we are better off to believe in him simply to find consolation (thereby skirting very close to Pascals chestnut). This insight is remarkable only insomuch as that it is a paper-thin argument that has managed to find such wide acceptance among the fans of armchair religion. In fact, I am amazed that so many believers would happily agree to reduce their god to just another `choice' on the Big Buffet of Beliefs.<br /><br />In his happy enthusiasm to present the reader with two choices: Belief in the `better story' or choose the `dry yeastless factuality' -what on earth does that really mean? - of the real event. Any reasonably able thinker would see that there are really numerous other choices to make, not just the two the author would like us to choose from. Among them, the fact that despite Martel's contempt for agnosticism it is actually a quite reasonable position to take and his volatile hate of `fence sitting' is a markedly biased and immature point of view. Another choice would be to explore the impact of religion in more depth than the airbrushed versions on display here.<br /><br />What of the choice to understand. The awe of understanding is by far superior to the awe of ignorance (as Douglas Adams put it). If Pi's story is a mundane one, then so be it. There are enough wonders to be enjoyed without garish embellishment to gild the lily.<br /><br />Furthermore, Martel states that belief and atheism both go as far as logic and reason would take them and then make the same leap of faith. This is demonstrably and patently false since atheism draws on thousands of years of human experience and evidence of gods non-existence, then simply takes one more step to conclude that he does not, in fact exist. Just like all the thousands of other gods we created. Believers, however start from the point of believe and then try to force experience and reason to fit their beliefs. A fools errand, since they invariably end up cherrypicking the bits of their religion that best suit reason and circumvents logic. Entirely not the same thing in any way whatsoever. The fact that Martel doesn't see -or worse, blithely ignores the universe size difference betweento two- places him firmly in the Deepak Chopra/ Alain de Button class of fuzzily muddled pseudo-intellectual showmen.<br /><br />In short, Life of Pi lies closer to The Da Vinci Code than concisely elegant fables like Old man and The Sea or Animal Farm.to two- places him firmly in the Deepak Chopra/ Alain de Button class of fuzzily muddled pseudo-intellectual showmen.<br /><br />In short, Life of Pi lies closer to The Da Vinci Code than concisely elegant fables like Old man and The Sea or Animal Farm.

B006VC3LES|5.0|Roman Polanski's genius is questionable. Whether or not he does possess a certain cinematic genius is neither urgent or necessary to conclude. His body of work speaks for itself, his 1974 magnum opus speaks for its decade, reality in the face of theatricality, and for the sanctity of the cinema itself. Now although it is not completely justifiable to prove whether Polanski is or is not a genius, it is rather important to discuss the life of this idolized and wanted man. Roman Polanski was born in the year 1933 in Paris to Polish parents. When his mother and father were forced into concentration camps for being Jewish, Polanski was faced with the unmeasurable difficulty of surviving the Holocaust on his own. His Mother ultimately fell victim to the horrors of Auschwitz. If that's not enough, if you would so melodramatically, fast forward to the year of 1969. To the Summer Of Love. To the end of the small portion of Polanski's life he would later recall as the happiest he's ever had. He had a beautiful, talented, and adoring wife. Her name was Sharon Tate. The baby inside of her, she and Roman would never name. In August of 1969 the Manson Family laid slain to Sharon Tate and the baby inside of her, both terrorizing and taunting, the darkest chapter of Polanski's life was marooned with notoriety and fear. It was only a few years after the death of his wife where Roman would leave his mark on the world forever.<br /><br />Chinatown flows with a lavishly perfected Noir tone, and I should add here, the film is the Neo Noir archetype, the best of its kind and one of the best that Film Noir carries in its respective canon. Also, whenever one sets out to write about Chinatown, one cannot understate the delicacy, the supreme handling of pacing which is to be found in this particular film. Not before, and not since, has pacing been so well thought out, so marvelously calculated, and devilishly crafted.<br /><br />Jack Nicholson and Faye Dunaway stand immortal in this film, bellowing through the tenement halls inside of every dirty city confined within every Noir film in rotation. If Jack Nicholson and Faye Dunaway stand luminous as Kings of their respective Kingdom, then John Huston stands apart; shrouded in darkness, transparent, eager, vicious, immediate. John Huston's 'Noah Cross' exists, alright, and the most dangerous thing about the 'Noah Cross's of the world is that the evil within them is otherwise ordinary, bottomless, and unstoppable. Robert Towne's script is simply impeccable. Flawless storytelling accompanied by snappy dialogue, and a climax that cuts deeper than that of documentary reality.<br /><br />But it is Polanski who is the true star of this picture. You can see him in every shot, peering in through the lens, and directly into your heart. You can taste him in every dry patch of dialogue, smell him in the midst of gun smoke, and fear him as the tension culminates into the wake of tragedy. It is there, in the tragedy, he dares you to hope for the best, he wants to feel safe because films make you feel comfortable. He takes the trust that he'd earned from the audience, and crushes it, oh, and he makes sure it stings. He makes sure it hurts. And in in the aftermath of the his final act, his final betrayal, the ultimate tragedy, he makes sure you can feel him. He leaves you in pain. He leaves you guilty in satisfaction. Knowing that tragedy is the most universal of luxuries, Polanski makes that perfectly clear, and for the first time, his voice is heard, his war cry. Coherent and horrifying.<br /><br />While Rosemary's Baby is my favorite Polanski film, Chinatown has always been a close second. Rosemary's Baby has also been selected by Criterion (along with other Polanski classics like Knife In The Water and Repulsion). While Rosemary's Baby has been given the care and respect it deserves with a stunning Blu Ray transfer by Criterion, Chinatown is in a sad state. A film this important should be treated as such.<br /><br />If you have never seen the film before, buy the Blu Ray. If you already own the DVD, there is no reason for you to upgrade. Wait until the Devil gets his due.

Some cinephiles or historians may feel like pointing elsewhere, to Jean Renoir for example, protesting that he is truly the father of the French New Wave, I disagree entirely. The French New Wave was a product of the kindred spirits and talents of Godard and Truffaut. And no other film represents the French New Wave more coherently than Jean-Luc Godard's dazzling 1960 picture, 'Breathless'. Unorthodox and uninhibited. Raw and experimental. Godard broke every rule in the book, disavowed the laws of cinema, and scribed a new rule book, one where the pages are blank, and possibilities are endless. The story is so simple I can summarize the entire film in half of a sentence, but I refuse to give you or Godard the satisfaction. What counts in Breathless is not the story, but how the story is told. The very definition of 'cool', the film is robust in creativity, and exuberant in its flaws, and passionate about its reckless behavior. Self aware and rebellious. If there was no Breathless, there would have been no Tarantino, if there was no French New Wave, the streak of genius that ran through the nineties and 2000s wouldn't have existed. Before Breathless, it was believed that films took money, that you had to find yourself in a place of authority to make a film. Breathless broke this misconception. Breathless makes you want to run outside and make the film of your life. B00902MCVY|2.0|With the incredible hype surrounding this album of 6 years in the making, I went in with as little expectation as possible. I hoped for the minimum, something at least as good as Wintersun's debut self title. With only a touch more than 25 min of actual metal songs with few guitar solos and little emphasis on guitarwork, the album leaves me unsatisfied especially compared to the length of this album. There is only 1 guitar solo, and you wait 33 min to get to it. It then only lasts 30 seconds and doesn't blow you away. However, this album while not blowing me away certainly isn't terrible either. It is worth a listen, but do not expect this to sound much like Wintersun's debut. I never have the urge to turn it off, and it does grow on you as you hear it a few times. However, many times I feel like I missed a section so I go back and find that I missed nothing at all. The same couldn't be said about their debut. 7 min into Wintersun I am more satisfied as a metalhead than I am listening to all of Time I. Track 2, Winter Madness has more guitar work than all of Time I with an excellent chill inducing 1 minute solo.<br /><br />For listening to this album, I used two setups to listen to the album several times on each:<br />Main Setup: Lilith Audio Player ASIO -> E-MU 0404 USB -> Mini^3 Amp -> BeyerDynamic DT990 250 ohm<br />Casual Setup: Foobar2000 -> Syba SD-CM-UAD USB -> Sennheiser HD555<br /><br />For the audiophile, the sound quality is excellent. Everything is well mastered and instruments and vocals are balanced nicely without one taking over and drowning out the other.<br /><br />Drums are the overall star here. Having seen Kai Hato filling in for Kreator's drummer live less than 30 days ago, this is no surprise as his drumming skills are to put it simply, beast. They for me are the most interesting part of the album. The other aspects, the synth aspects while fun and create a beautiful grand and majestic atmosphere, become monotonous at times and leave me wishing for more variation. Too much repetition for too long without enough change. If more Bach influence had made its way into the synth work, this album would have risen to a whole different level. Or instead of more synths at times, simply more solid guitarwork would have been better. There is only so many times bells really need to heard.<br /><br />When Time Fades Away is a great intro for the album. It sets the mood and general pace to expect, bringing in some interesting Japanese influence using likely a Zither and/or Shamisen to create peaceful aw-inspiring imagery of falling cherry blossoms while surrounded by a serene body of water. However, these Japanese influences are not to be heard much again other than briefly in the very beginning of the first epic track until the final epic track, Time. It is quite a shame as hearing Wintersun describe Time I over the last year especially left me expecting a bit more influence than what was found in the good but ultimately throwaway intro and then the repeating similar sound later in the album very sparsely. More varied uses of the Japanese strings, more than simply one Japanese inspired melody would have been interesting and worthwhile. There is a loss in potential in not utilizing the Zither much more through the album. I expected it more present in the main track of this album, Sons of Winter and Stars.<br /><br />Sons of Winter and Stars is an excellent passionate, though not quite a masterpiece, track that stands out and sent a few chills through my body. The background vocals that are introduced at 1:20 are unexpected, a choir voice reminiscent of almost heavenly latin influences. It serves as contrast with the harsh vocals that are introduced moments later. With more Japanese influenced strings just seconds ago this was not what I thought I'ld hear so early on, but it worked well enough and adds to the atmosphere. However, this is nearly the last heard of it. At 5:18 in or so, things become astral. Sounds of space ships and flash back inducing stereo effects. Clearly this is what was intended. This is the moment in the album where you have now reached space. At times the synths act awkward and some odd choices arise, such as why a tambourine was bothered to be included when so much is already going on symphonic wise. The use of trumpet in this song is okay. Reminds me much of Turisas, but executed just a little lesser. However, there's 8 min left to the song. I feel at about the 7 min mark, the song could have ended. Instead, it goes acoustic for a bit and then into what I'll look at as phase 2 of the song. This phase of the song reminds me greatly of Ensiferum, especially when they add the guitar back in a few min later at almost the 10 min mark and start giving us some guitarwork that is craved. It has that storybook feel that Ensiferum does well.<br /><br />The middle tracks before the final epic track are just okay. They showcase the softer side of Wintersun and have a certain warmth that's admirable to hear, but given the plenty of lulls in Sons of Winter and Stars, it isn't necessary to give the listener such a break from the intensity that is sought from melodic death. I have a hard time calling either of these tracks very metal, they are not complex enough and are more similar to symphonic hard rock. After hearing it a few times, there isn't much more to discover or find. It becomes boring. If Sons of Winter and Stars didn't end at the end and instead built into Land of Snow and Sorrow continuing on with the album building back into the intensity typical of melodic death, much more could have been achieved- not taking a break when the momentum was just getting started. The power influences are mildly interesting, but like Ensiferum's Iron it is not Wintersun's forte. Utilizing the violin better in this track and really carrying a strong melody could have at least given something to truly come back for. Five min into Land of Snow and Sorrow and I'm ready for it to end, but it doesn't. It continues on for a few more min, but nothing really noteworthy is heard beyond a couple minutes of a melodic guitar repeating the same melody a few times ultimately feeling like too little too late. It is possible that with these two tracks, the Japanese string instruments are being used. However, because of the use of the synth as well it does not sound much different than an acoustical guitar. Darkness and Frost's following acoustical interlude only confuses this further.  By the end of Land of Snow and Sorrow you are quite peaceful and the track ends very quietly. More slow and lightness isn't necessary, not unless the final epic song is going to be such a major contrast that could be the only possible reason for this lightness.<br /><br />However it isn't. The final track Time is tame in general, spending more time with bells and pretty synth sounds than serious guitar and drumwork. The first minute of the song is not bad, but then the lead guitar disappears until several more minutes in for a brief moment before giving way to bells and synths once more. The heavy use of synths throughout the album is not necessarily a negative, however the lack of variation in the synths ends up with them feeling repetitive. The only solo of the album takes place 6 min in that lasts exactly 30 seconds. It's a solid solo, but nothing spectacular. Certainly not long enough and great enough to have waited 33 min for a solo. This last track musically really ends at 9:15. It somehow goes on for a couple minutes longer with more Japanese inspired strings that ties in with the intro for an attempt at coming full circle. This last Japanese inspired segment isn't really needed, the short piano solo that preceded this last out segment was actually a surprise. More of pianowork would have been well received throughout this final track.<br /><br />With a great amount of effort and time, Wintersun has produced a unique album with Time I. It's more of an intro to a great album than a great album on its own. With only two metal tracks, it leaves great expectations for Time II to pick up the slack Time I created and further develop their sophomore album. Time does fade away in this album, waiting and waiting for interesting moments.

B0071XO8RA|4.0|Okay, this turned out to be a pleasant surprise. I found this to be an entertaining read in the 3.5-4 star range. Other than having an amazing first name, Howey managed to find a way to make the usual, post appocalyptic scenarios fresh even using old themes.<br /><br />Best of all, is the world building, which, followed a lot of \\"tropes\\" yet, managed to be different than what I expected, in a good way. The ladders, stairwells, pipe and cable filled passageways of the \\"Silos\\" reminded me of life aboard ship while I was in the Navy. What would it be like to have to live on something the size of an aircraft carrier and farm out basic rations to the point of an almost socialist all for one, and one for all mentality. Barter for goods and being angry as hell at Supply...the cheap bastards... not to mention IT, the devil in any machine these days. Silo life had a very unique feel as a basis for the world where a story is set and the characters live. It shouldn't, there's nothing new about living on space ships and under domes (ala-Logan's Run) but some how, it seemed new here.<br /><br />I also like the semi-capitalist-semi-socialist-semi-democratic-semi-oligargaical (if that's a word) society. There is a lot of life mirrored in these stories. Stratified society based on class, colour (of uniform) and skill sets. The dark facination with \\"Cleanings\\" and the fact that Howey didn't stop with the \\"You damned bloody Apes, you burned it up!\\" story theme, taking it a step further.<br /><br />The characters were hit or miss, I liked Knox, I liked Julliette (more on her later) I liked the crazy old man in the small workshop in Mechanical, and the major villain. The Sad but strangely sweet story between the mayor and sheriff brought the sting of a tear to my usually grumpy eyes...of course it was kind of dusty that day!... and the sad story of the \\"old Sheriff\\" and his demise turned out to be a real heartbreaker too. I wasn't so keen on the Juliette's love interest and I thought the New-new sheriff (after Julliette) got short shifted, since he became so pivitol.<br /><br />Plot wise, I'd have liked to see more of a connection between Solo and Shirely and the gang in Mechanical. Many of those were billed to have a huge role in Juliette's life yet, got too little \\"airtime\\" to see it.<br /><br />The best character was Juliette. Strong woman, pretty, able to love, old enough to know better, with a very practical sense of hope and strength that simply put, said, \\"you live just one day at a time, no more, no less. Get done what needs to be done today, let tomorrow's jobs be done tomorrow.\\" mentality and a \\"just put one foot in front of the other\\" sense of courage that carried her through her adventures in Silo 18. She could be physical (a fighter) the way Howey wrote her, but, instead, she thought problems out, like a mechanic or a carpenter, then went to work to solve the problem and get the thing running...what ever it is.<br /><br />The villain was suitably villainous too. A good bad guy who had a bit part, but was written well into it.<br /><br />The short comings in plot and some of the characters is off set, in my opinion, by one thing. No matter how glum things got, how desparate, how depressing, all of the major characters found something to hold on to, some sense of hope. They didn't long to live outside, rather, then longed to make the lives that they have work so they can be happy when they get the chance. No whining about the outside, but plenty of hope for the future in an otherwise bleak and hopeless setting.<br /><br />That's just good stuff. Happyness isn't a state of mind, you have to build an environment that it can bloom in, when it's time for happiness to bloom.<br /><br />Good book<br /><br />Warnings, violence, but generally, nothing that YA to OA readers couldn't enjoy. 1439127700|5.0|How do men who have been President of the United States of America spend their time in retirement? One of the possibilities is that they take an active role in the Presidents Club (the Club). In their book unsurprisingly titled 'The Presidents Club', Nancy Gibbs and Michael Duffy give an account of the Club's activities and how it behaved from President Truman's close relationship with ex-president Hoover to the present era of President Obama. The club is small due to the scarcity of retired presidents. Bill Clinton had the support of one of the largest with access to Nixon,Ford,Carter,Reagan and G.H.W Bush. This Club is completely informal in that meetings are at the whim of the incumbent president who usually sees members individually. There are no rules or regulations generally associated with clubs and societies.<br /><br />It is clear that the overriding function of the Club is the preservation and enhancement of the office of the Presidency. This explains how retired presidents can operate with the President of the day despite differences in political persuasion and personal likes or dislikes. The authors examine the critical issues that bedevilled each president and how the Club participated or otherwise in their resolution. The help ranged from providing a sounding-board for a lonely man to carrying out major projects with critical international sensitivities. In this regard one has only to read how ex-president Jimmy Carter saw his resonsibilities.<br /><br />This book is emminently readable,exciting in parts and written as you would expect from two editors at 'Time'. It also has strong academic credentials with an impressive research foundation. It is a beautiful example of accessible modern history. It is interesting that there is no discussion regarding the place of the Club, which has no electoral or other mandate, within the democratic proccess. In parliamentary systems there is nothing so past as a past prime minister. They tend to languish in retirement some destined for oblivion.<br /><br />Everyone with the slightest interest in American politics should read this book. It is redolent with the atmosphere of power. To outsiders the American political system is something of a mystery and this book is a fascinating account of a little known piece of this complex jigsaw. Long live the Presidents Club!

He starts out with an underdog who's underestimated and always wins in the end, which is of course, that he stops being a lawyer.  haha.<br /><br />But seriously, I'm not giving away the plot because the story starts out with a forty-something, African-American lawyer who's spent the last five years of his life in a 'club-med' prison for white collar offenders.  Malcom is too smart to serve all 10 years of his sentence, and when a local supreme court judge is murdered, along with his secretary, guess who has the answer as to who-done-it?  Certainly not the federal government, the ones responsible for ruining Malcom's life with trumped up charges of racketeering.<br /><br />How Malcom beats the government drones at their own game is both awe-inspiring and hilarious.  Everything seemed pretty cut and dry in the middle of the book and I found myself ready to relax my break-neck speed-reading marathon, but of course this is John Grisham, master story teller, and when he throws a new hook in the story, I find myself once again unable to put the book down.<br /><br />I love how Grisham's hero's come alive in his stories. These are real people with all their strengths and foibles, totally relateable and sympathetic.  Another thing I love, is learning about the life of lawyers, and in The Racketeer, I learned way too much about what it would be like to be in prison.  I have to wonder if Grisham had himself locked up for a few months to be able to write so realistically about our hell-hole of a prison system.<br /><br />But my favorite thing about Grisham's novels, is his compassion for the underdog. Grisham's novels at least point that fact out, and then show us ways to even the scales so to speak.<br /><br />The Racketeer is another fascinating romp through the legal world, this time showing how the government that was put in place to protect us, can sometimes be our worst enemy.  Grisham once again writes the perfect book for a long airplane ride, beach or anywhere when you need some fun escapism.  He's never disappointed me yet.protect us, can sometimes be our worst enemy. 1451662432|4.0|I was weaned on the giants of the `80s action movies: Stallone, Seagal, Van Damme, Lundgren and of course, Schwarzenegger. I can in fact remember the first time I saw an Arnie film, when my babysitter decided to put on The Terminator and at a pre-school age, I was confronted with the sight of this behemoth of a man, carving a path of carnage through LA. From then on in, I was a fan.<br />When Total Recall: My Unbelievably True Life Story the autobiography of Arnold Schwarzenegger landed on my doormat, I felt like a kid at Christmas!<br /><br />The life story of Arnold Schwarzenegger is truly remarkable. In his own words, \\"...what are the odds... for an Austrian farm boy to come to America and become the greatest bodybuilding champion of all time, to get in the movie business, marry a Kennedy, and then get elected governor of the biggest state of the United States...\\"<br />From that brief quotation, the reader can see there is certainly plenty of material for him to cover and this is demonstrated by the fact that you're halfway through the book before any real discussion of The Terminator: a movie that was made almost 30 years ago.<br /><br />At times, I was laughing uncontrollably at some of the stories in this book and most of the time, I couldn't help but read it and hear his voice in your head! Although much of what is narrated in the book is already well-known, getting Schwarzenegger's version of events is compelling, hearing about his childhood in Austria, his will to succeed in bodybuilding, how Conan the Barbarian brought him international stardom, how he was determined to succeed as Chairman of the President's Council on Physical Fitness and Sports; and many other tales that leave you wondering how one man at the age of 65 years old could fit so much into one lifetime.<br /><br />Arnold comes across as incredibly driven, highly competitive and quite calculating, sometimes to the point where I found myself losing a bit of respect for him. His psychological toying with other athletes on and off the stage were not what I would expect from a modern athlete but then again, Schwarzenegger really was a game-changer as far as the bodybuilding world was concerned.<br /><br />As the book progressed, I was worried that he would simply ignore the subject of his lovechild but in fact, my fears were allayed late on in the book. Despite this, he skirts over his own failings and misdemeanours such as affairs, alleged sexual harassment, drug use and steroid abuse. This may be understandable, given that Arnold is still a high profile individual, a role model to many and a man who still hopes that he can save his marriage to Maria Shriver.<br /><br />The section on Arnie's time in politics will not be to everyone's taste, especially those who are more interested in his bodybuilding and/or movie career. At times, it can be very dry and overly-descriptive about political processes; but it is also not without its highpoints and is peppered with references and anecdotes about famous politicians and celebrities alike. Schwarzenegger freely admits many of the mistakes he made while in office as the Governor of California but is keen to point out that he gave it his absolute all, just like every other career decision he has made.<br /><br />For me, as a fan, I don't regret reading this book. Much of the information contained therein is already common knowledge but the facts and circumstances that are already known are brought to life with Schwarzenegger's own stories and thoughts on events. The title of the book however, is only partly true; the latter half being correct in that yes, it is indeed an \\"unbelievably true life story\\"; but the title \\"Total Recall\\" is quite the misnomer, since I cannot help but feel much has been omitted deliberately from this book.<br />Ultimately, this book is much like the man himself: entertaining, larger than life, charismatic and unforgettable; but not without its flaws.ying with other athletes on and off the stage were not what I would expect from a modern athlete but then again, Schwarzenegger really was a game-changer as far as the bodybuilding world was concerned.<br /><br />As the book progressed, I was worried that he would simply ignore the subject of his lovechild but in fact, my fears were allayed late on in the book. There is the need to look askance at a less than believable plot, but in spite of the necessity to accept non logical events it is a captivating read.  Malcolm Bannister is an attorney caught up in a crime in which his physical presence causes him to be found guilty and sent to prison for 10 years.  It looks like in spite of his innocence he will have to serve the sentence and leave prison with his life ruined by the Federal government, his wife divorcing him, and no way to earn a living as a practicing lawyer.<br />  In the fifth year of his sentence,  Raymond Fawcett  a Federal Judge and his girl friend are killed and the safe in the Judge's house found open and emptied.  Malcolm knows who did it and negotiates with the government for his release and entrance into the witness protection program in return for information leading to the killer.  With the negotiation successful and ensuing events unfolding Grisham spins a tangled web with twists and turns that keep the reader going on with the book.  Predicting the ending is almost impossible, but within the realm of what is happening is logical, and rewarding.  Like his other books The Racketeer is not easy to put down, and it is possible for the reader to go along with the considerably less than logical sequence of events.

0812992784|5.0|There are so many things wrong with my title sentence that I hardly know where to begin. It is a quote from the Wikipedia article on The Satanic Verses, Rushdie's 1989 novel that became the focal point of the great conflict.<br /><br />I was completely engrossed by this nonfiction account of Salman Rushdie's years in hiding. This mild mannered writer, relatively unknown outside of literary circles, even after winning the 1981Booker Prize for Midnight's Children, was sentenced to death for writing a book, The Satanic Verses, in a fatwa by the dying Ayatollah Khomeni. Mr. Rushdie was demonized by many thousands of outraged Muslims, those tireless defenders of the religion of peace, witness to the banning and burning of his book and his own effigy, and the falling away of many ostensibly liberal western authors, some of whom used to be his friends. He persevered in living and writing, and obtained the best revenge.<br /><br />The fatwa stated that it was the duty of Muslims everywhere to kill Salman Rushdie, for writing a book which the Ayatollah thought was insulting to Islam (it was not actually about Islam, but never mind that). The British government assigned a special police squad to protect him, and he was a virtual prisoner for years, moving from one secret place to another, having limited contact with family and friends, denied public appearances, and even the further publication of his book, because people had a genuine fear of violence from even a distant association with the man or his book. This was not an example of that oxymoronic term islamophobia, because Islamic terrorists killed several people, bombed bookstores, and publically proclaimed the murder of an author as a sacred religious duty. It is telling to recall that in 1989 this was still shocking to the western world. At a Muslim youth conference in Bradford a 16 year old girl called for Rushdie to be stoned to death. The singer formerly known as Cat Stevens agreed that Rushdie \\"must be killed.\\" Several luminaries in literature and members of parliament stated that it was Rushdie's own fault for offending Muslims, because he should have known that they would respond with violence.<br /><br />Mr. Rushdie writes with a practiced and winning style, showing a wide ranging intellect, and a convincing command of language. The choice of the third person to narrate an autobiography is unusual, and it creates some distance from the author and his subject, distance that works here, because the tale is an emotional one, but he tells it with a sure and even hand. Sometimes he seems excessively defensive, taking great efforts to explain how a remark or a slight offended him, and I began to suspect that I might not like the man if I met him in person. Nevertheless, he has been the recipient of extended public abuse, which was arbitrary and unjustifiable. In his situation, I would be defensive too.<br /><br />This might qualify as a cautionary tale, but of the wrong kind. Great Britain and the west have progressed further along the road to capitulation in the face of violence from offended Muslim extremists, aided and abetted by those who used to champion freedom. We have learned little from the sad tale of Mr. Rushdie, which is greatly to our peril. All around the world, our freedoms are under attack.veral luminaries in literature and members of parliament stated that it was Rushdie's own fault for offending Muslims, because he should have known that they would respond with violence.<br /><br />Mr. Rushdie writes with a practiced and winning style, showing a wide ranging intellect, and a convincing command of language. B004HW7JH4|4.0|On his ubiquitous commentaries for the DVD set of season 5 of MAD MEN, the series creator Matt Weiner repeatedly says the theme for the season was \\"Every man for himself\\"; however, this season of the show is for once dominated much more heavily by the women than it is by the men. Very few of the major male characters (including Don Draper) are in as proactive positions as they have been in past seasons, and instead we see the women as making the more dominant choices in the season, from Betty's long-deferred decision about advancing her career opportunities, to Joan's unexpected choices about what to do with her career and her marriage, and to the many surprising choices of Don's beautiful new bride Megan (Jessica Pare) to settle into married life. Don's ex-wife Betty is of course here again too, and makes some decisions of her own about getting her own life after her unhappiness at seeing her husband get remarried (which results in January Jones wearing prosthetics to make her seem quite fat, partially to cover the actress's real-life pregnancy), and the most dominant minor characters are two formidable mothers-in-law, Betty's (played by Pamela Dunlap--a favorite from last season), and Don's (played by Julia Ormond).<br /><br />\\"Why do [women] get to make all of the decisions?,\\" Pete Campbell whines about his flirtation with the depressed bride of a fellow commuter. The answer, as this season seems to show, is that the men seem incapable by this point in the series of making their lives happen: all of their agency seems to have been drained away, not just by the onslaught of the real events of 1966 (the ongoing civil rights struggles, the mass murders of Richard Speck and Charles Whitman, the youth movement), but by their own inabilities to take command. Repeatedly the men in the show are seen at a real crossroads, watching their own rare decisions turn sour (as Lane Pryce does, in the series' most tragic subplot). Certainly this was not what would have been predicted for the path for this show to take when the show started, when the women were mostly trying to carve whatever small space they could in a Manhattan and suburban world dominated by greedy and ambitious men. But one of the best things about this series has consistently been its inability to remain stale: it keeps moving in different directions, and Don's new marriage to Megan (and their fabulous high-rise apartment) really takes this season in a new direction. The acting, like the writing, is, as usual, consistently high: Jon Hamm and Megan Pare do well with the biggest roles of the season, but they're nearly outshone by Christina Hendricks and Jared Harris, who are given here more than they've ever had to do before.<br /><br />This DVD set comes with better packaging than the last few, although some of the special features seem pretty superfluous: a history of Daylight's Saving Time seemed unnecessary, and the mini-profile of the painter Giorgio Di Chirico (which is here because he inspired the season's unremarkable poster) also doesn't seem terribly essential. The commentaries are always great, although Matt Weiner always dominates whichever ones he shares; still, since he knows more about the show than anyone else, this seems fair.

B009CVELHG|5.0|Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer [MGM}, the premier and most profitable studio of the 1930s and Irving Grant Thalberg {IGT}, the Studio's creative vice president in charge of production and greatest production executive of that {or any other} era with this film showed Hollywood {then and now} how to make an engrossing all star film that remains untarnished by time 80 + years after it's debute as one of filmdom's greatest drama's.<br /><br />IGT was the driving force in pushing the production forward with 5 major stars {most\\"A Class\\" product in 1932 had 2 stars}, fine-tuning the script, casting the picture and working with director Edmund Goulding to create the nuiances / details that create the tension, humor, pathos and drama within the screenplay taking place in a luxury hotel located in Berlin in pre- 1933 Germany before the Nazi storm.<br /><br />The all-star cast includes: Greta Garbo - Gruinskaya, the fading ballerina disillusioned with life, John Barrymore - Baron von Geiger, charming, broke and desperate for money, Joan Crawford - Flaemmchen, the stenographer halfway \\"decent\\", halfway \\"on the make\\" - which of these impluses will prevail? , Wallace Beery - General Director Preysing a hypocritical businessman facing ruin unless he pulls off an impossible business merger and blessed with a \\"roving eye\\", Lionel Barrymore - Otto Kringelein ,a dying little clerk out for one \\"last fling\\" and in support of the stars: veteran characther actors Lewis Stone as the scarred, forlorn Doctor Ottenschlag and Jean Hersholt playing Senf, the Grand Hotel's chief Porter \\"chained to his desk\\" with his pregnant wife expecting a nightmare delivery any second.<br /><br />The Grand Hotel, an art deco marvel courtesy of MGM resident genius art director Cedric Gibbon is the unbilled star of the story where the lives of these very different characthers will inter-twine and some of them will find {and some of them will find and lose} love, death, redemption, wealth, ruin, murder and happiness as their lives and problems criss-cross each other. While every performer gives of their best, two performances in particular stand out - Lionel Barrymore's dying clerk, timid, obseqious with a innate dignity that comes to the hotel to blow his life savings and gets much more than he bargained for and in stark contrast - Wallace Beery's arrogant, gross, ill- mannered boor complete with bristling Prussian haircut and Germanic accent AND YET is somehow still pathetic and vunerable - their performances \\"vis a vie\\" each other and the rest of the cast crackle and sparkle.<br /><br />Amoung the many individual bravura episolds /scenes in the film, three in particular stand out: The introduction of the principle players at the films beginning via closeups of them on the telephone all desperately explaining what the various issues that they needed to resolve and thru quick cuts, editing and cross-cutting of the various conversations it becomes more intense and hightens the tension of the scene AND it sets up the various sub-plots for the entire film. In a humorous aside - Kringlelein gleefully stumbling over, thru and around his hotel room while \\"drunk as a skunk\\" after sampling one to many \\"Louisiada Flips\\" after winning a fortune gambling at the Hotel's American Bar and the classic meeting of Garbo and Barrymore - The Baron skulking, hidden in her room, ready to commit a carefully planned robbery of Gruinskaya's pearls and upon seeing her beauty and desperation reveals himself and without missing a beat - goes from thief to her rescuer, protector and lover. This scene is beautifily played by both stars and it shows a romantic, understated sexual encounter [with the 1932 audience knowing exactly what will subsequently occur off screen} that is quite different from much of the 2012 -2013 U.S.A's TV and Hollywood view of sex and romance AND after Gruinskaya falls under the barons spell she no longer was in the Garbo \\"I vant to be alone\\" mode !!<br /><br />This film is the pre-ultimate example of MGM Studio Product at it's apex - a seamless fusion of star power, glossy production values, sensitive direction, tight editing and a scenario that is compelling and entertaining. At the absolute bottom of the Great Depression in 1932 \\"Grand Hotel\\" was the highest grossing film taking in over 4 million dollars worldwide {when a movie ticket sold from 10 to 35 cents} and winning the Academy Award for best picture.<br /><br />You will not see Irving Thalberg's name on any of the credits for \\"Grand Hotel\\" BUT he was the main force in envisioning, creating and bringing to the screen one of his and MGM's crowning achievements of great product made during MGM's \\"Golden Era\\" of 1930-1940. Doctor Ottenschalg's periodic weary refrain: \\"Grand Hotel, Grand Hotel, people come, people go, nothing ever happens !\\" was definitely shown to be a statement that was \\"just a tad incorrect\\" !!. This is a terrific movie and I give it an enthusiastic 5 Stars  - Highly Recommended.
