1451695195|5.0|Having read dozens of books about near-death experiences (NDEs) and other phenomena strongly suggesting the survival of consciousness after death, I didn't read this book in search of \\"proof\\" of \\"heaven.\\"  I had already come to the conclusion that the evidence for survival, if not providing absolute proof, at least meets the \\"beyond a reasonable doubt\\" standard of our legal system.  However, just as the orthodox religious person needs to renew his or her faith by reading his/her \\"good book\\" over and over again and then attending weekly services to hear someone discuss the teachings of the book - discussions probably heard dozens of times before - I find a certain renewal and revitalization in reading newer accounts of NDEs.  Yes, the stories are often similar, as one might expect them to be if they are to be considered evidential, but there is usually enough variety to make the stories interesting, informative, intriguing, and inspirational, what I call the \\"four I's.\\"  This book exceeded my expectations, for each of those four I's.<br /><br />Because Dr. Eben Alexander, the author and experiencer, is a neurosurgeon who was for the most part a materialist with little interest in the subject of NDEs before his own NDE, this story has even more credibility than most, although the pseudoskeptics, in all their wisdom, were attempting to discredit the author as delusional and the victim of wishful thinking even before the book was released.  \\"Like many other scientific skeptics, I refused to even review the data relevant to the questions concerning these phenomena,\\" Alexander tells of his own pre-NDE mindset. \\"I prejudged the data, and those providing it, because my limited perspective failed to provide the foggiest notion of how such things might actually happen.\\"  He now sees such people \\"who know the truth without needing to look at the facts\\" as willfully ignorant.<br /><br />\\"Brilliant, vibrant, ecstatic, stunning,\\" Alexander describes the \\"new world\\" he found himself in after his physical body and brain completely shut down, admitting, as so many others have, that  human language and ideas fail when one attempts to translate celestial visions and experiences into terrestrial imagery and verbiage.  \\"We taint, with out insufficient descriptions, their truly awesome nature,\\" he offers, stressing his belief that what he experienced was \\"more real than the house I sat in, more real than the logs burning in the fireplace.\\"<br /><br />Alexander examines the usual NDE debunking theories, including endogenous glutamate blockade, REM intrusion, \\"DMT dump,\\" the \\"reboot phenomenon,\\" and \\"feigned-death\\" strategies, dismissing them one by one.  Everything he experienced suggested higher, not lower, brain functioning.  \\"The more I read of the `scientific' explanations of what NDEs are, the more I was shocked by their transparent flimsiness,\\" he states, going on to say that after studying the subject matter following his NDE, he began to realize his was \\"a technically near-impeccable near-death experience.\\"<br /><br />In one of the final chapters, Alexander tells of stories he had heard from patients before his experience - stories which he wrote off as fantasy, but which he now saw very possibly real.  He also mentions a very interesting story related to him by one of his colleagues involving \\"terminal lucidity,\\" in which the colleague's dying father, who had been demented, became lucid and in last moments alive.<br /><br />In his 1974 Pulitzer prize-winning book, \\"The Denial of Death,\\" anthropologist Ernest Becker stated that the fear of death haunts the human mind like nothing else. Most people deal with this fear by repressing it - burying it deep in the subconscious and busying themselves with mundane activities. And then, one day, perhaps when it becomes apparent that their days are numbered, those repressed anxieties relating to death begin welling up into the consciousness.  They proceed to live their final years under a dark and increasingly foreboding shadow.  For the most part, the muddled information provided by orthodox religion offers little relief, little comfort.  But books like this one serve to erase that fear and allow the person to embrace death, living his or her final years with a certain optimism.  I am grateful to Dr. Alexander for writing it and intend to buy a few extra copies to give as Christmas presents.reasingly foreboding shadow. B000002J03|5.0|Sure, Led Zeppelin might have rushed to record it during the downtime of their first stateside tours, but \\"II\\" is still a positively masterful album. Hugely influential, too -- it would go on to inspire trillions of bands, and thousands of just plain imitators/copycats. And sure, the template for several of these songs might be bluesy rock and roll; but overall, \\"II\\" packs a very heavy, straightforwardly driving, and viscerally-satisfying sonic force. This is mostly thanks to the fact that Jimmy Page comes up with some of the absolute finest, biggest, and most infectious riffs of his recording career all throughout these nine songs. And, as cuts like \\"The Lemon Song\\" prove, he is no stranger to wailing guitar solos, too.<br /><br />\\"Whole Lotta Love\\" is an instant and immediately recognizable classic opener. The song is blues-rock at its core (heck, it even features a Theremin!); but it does feature heavy, metallic musicianship -- Page pounds out a wealth of simple yet very inventive, galloping (and always really loud) riffs. \\"What Is And What Should Never Be\\" is another very bluesy number, and it is not as heavy as its predecessor; and, in fact, is curiously quiet and ambient (at least in part). \\"The Lemon Song\\" returns the album back to its heavy roots, though, with brutal riffs and propulsive, muscular bass lines. (And the above-mentioned solo section, too.) \\"Thank You\\" anchors pretty, feather-lite acoustic flourishes with ominous bass lines, and tops it all off with Robert Plant's trademark wail. \\"Heartbreaker\\" is another undisputed classic, a heavy, big riff rocker with a solid, grumbling bass bottom, and another excellent, epic guitar solo section. And dig the song's great chorus, too -- it is one that you cannot get out of your head even if you wanted to! \\"Living Loving Maid (She's Just A Woman)\\" features similarly guitar-driven rhythms in the verses that flow into another killer, and instinctively memorable chorus.  \\"Ramble On,\\" again sees the band exploring experimental, progressive territory, with jazzy, jangly acoustic guitars and nicely melodic bass work. The set-closing \\"Bring It On Home\\" opens with a wailing, Jethro Tull-esque bluegrass-y sounding harmonica; but the guitars slam their way onto the scene around a minute and forty-five seconds in, thus transforming the song into another big riff-driven hard rocker.<br /><br />However, it is \\"Moby Dick\\" that takes the indisputable cake for being the album's epic standout. Almost deserving of a whole new review all by itself, \\"Moby Dick\\" begins on a heavy note, with hard-rocking, even mildly doomy, downtuned guitars driving the intro. A shredding solo crops up, too -- but it is all just a lead-in to one of the best drum solos of all time. Indeed, percussionist John Bonham sure shows off his impressive dexterity, here, by creating a fantastic, nearly three-minute long solo that is at times jazzy-sounding and at other times thundering. But this drum solo is always terrifically deft, bouncy, rollicking, insanely memorable, and historically epic.<br /><br />1971's \\"IV\\" is still the crown jewel in Led Zeppelin's discography, but in no way should that take anything away from this album. \\"II\\" is one of the most influential and quintessential heavy metal recordings of all-time. And no music collection should be without it.experimental, progressive territory, with jazzy, jangly acoustic guitars and nicely melodic bass work. The set-closing \\"Bring It On Home\\" opens with a wailing, Jethro Tull-esque bluegrass-y sounding harmonica; but the guitars slam their way onto the scene around a minute and forty-five seconds in, thus transforming the song into another big riff-driven hard rocker.<br /><br />However, it is \\"Moby Dick\\" that takes the indisputable cake for being the album's epic standout. Almost deserving of a whole new review all by itself, \\"Moby Dick\\" begins on a heavy note, with hard-rocking, even mildly doomy, downtuned guitars driving the intro. 1423389409|5.0|This review is about the Audiobook version. I have listened to several epic fantasy series on audiobook, and I must say the KingKiller Chronicles audiobook versions are one of the best, if not best fantasy series audiobooks out there.<br /><br />Obviously, the audiobook reader is what makes or breaks the audiobook, and sadly i have listened to some readers that destroy the book entirely. Nick Podehl, the reader for the KingKiller Chronicles, is absolutely amazing, perhaps even better than the readers for The Wheel of Time series (whom I enjoy).<br /><br />Here is a quick list of what makes Nick Podehl an excellent story teller.<br /><br />1. Podehl has a great variety of voices for the various characters, many of which are quite diverse. His voices are consistent for each character and for how each character ought to sound in terms of nationalities, accents, age, gender, etc. The voices also fit in easily with my imagination; they did not clash and make me think \\"that is not how that character ought to sound\\". If there is an old wise mystical man, I have ideas and bias about how he should sound (Gandalf, Dumbledore, Zedd from the sword of truth series, etc.), and if he does not sound the way I imagine then it is noticed.<br /><br />His many voices add a new depth to the overall story, for not only do you get to experience the wonderful world that Rothfuss has created, but you also get to hear how voices change throughout this world, through Podehl's own imagination and talent. In fact, several times I knew what nationality a character was through the voice of the reader (although it was likely mentioned earlier in the book).<br /><br />2. None of Podehl's voices are annoying. Annoying voices are the most devastating flaw an audiobook reader can have. Not only do annoying voices make the book less enjoyable, but you are constantly thinking of how much you dislike the voice, instead of listening to the story. All of Podehl's voices are easy on the ear and enjoyable. Even characters with accents because of their nationality are easy to understand, and never did I have to re-listen to a section because I did not understand what was said.<br /><br />3. Podehl is a competent, intelligent reader. He knows his stuff, and he knows the story very well, down to its bones. His tones of voice are spot on, letting you hear the words the way they are meant to be said. He knows the context of each word and all the complexities of each words meaning. He knows the characters and how they ought to act. This adds even more depth to the story, giving you insights into the characters emotions and meanings behind each word that you might miss by simply reading them. At no point did I think \\"that is definitely not how the sentence is meant to be read.\\"<br /><br />4. Along with competence, Podehl's pronunciation of unfamiliar words and names gave me no issues. Although I have read the book in paperback format, I did not actually look up words and check his pronunciations against my own imagined pronunciations of the word. Still, I found no problems with them. (In comparison, I have listened to a book with two readers, where they pronounced a name quite different from each other. It took me a long time to even realize they were talking about the same character!).<br /><br />5. Finally, Podehl's voice fits Kvothe perfectly. I can't remember if it took me some getting used to, but the voice is exactly how I imagine Kvothe should sound. It is a very enjoyable voice, easy to listen to and understand, and I cannot imagine it sounding any other way.<br /><br />If you enjoy fantasy, and like to listen to audiobooks, the KingKiller Chronicles would be my number one recommendation. Not only is Nick Podehl fantastic, but the KingKiller Chronicles is one of my favorite fantasy series out there, and I would highly recommend it to anyone who enjoys delving into fantastical worlds with secrets and mysteries, love and loss, fairytales and legends, and characters whoyou can come to know and love.<br /><br />Yes, some people have issues with the books, but I enjoyed them without end. Above all else, I read fantasy for enjoyment, and as long as you go into it looking for adventure and wonder, you cannot possibly dislike these books. Perhaps the author gets side tracked along the way, but that is likely because he was so intensely into the story himself that it never even crossed his mind. Without a doubt, 5 star.
